**Earth** is the third [planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet "Planet") from the [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun") and the only [astronomical object](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_object "Astronomical object") known to [harbor life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability "Planetary habitability"). This is enabled by Earth being an [ocean world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_world "Ocean world"), the only one in the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System") sustaining liquid [surface water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_water "Surface water"). Almost all of Earth's water is contained in its global ocean, covering [70.8%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_distribution_on_Earth "Water distribution on Earth") of [Earth's crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_crust "Earth's crust"). The remaining 29.2% of Earth's crust is land, most of which is located in the form of [continental](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continent "Continent")[landmasses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landmass "Landmass") within Earth's [land hemisphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_hemisphere "Land hemisphere"). Most of Earth's land is at least somewhat [humid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humid "Humid") and covered by vegetation, while large [ice sheets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_sheet "Ice sheet") at Earth's polar [polar deserts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_desert "Polar desert") retain more water than Earth's [groundwater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Groundwater "Groundwater"), lakes, rivers, and [atmospheric water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vapor#In_Earth's_atmosphere "Water vapor") combined. Earth's crust consists of slowly moving [tectonic plates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tectonic_plates "Tectonic plates"), which interact to produce mountain ranges, [volcanoes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcano "Volcano"), and earthquakes. [Earth has a liquid outer core](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_outer_core "Earth's outer core") that generates a [magnetosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetosphere "Magnetosphere") capable of deflecting most of the destructive [solar winds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind") and [cosmic radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_radiation "Cosmic radiation").

Earth has [a dynamic atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth "Atmosphere of Earth"), which sustains Earth's surface conditions and protects it from most [meteoroids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteoroid "Meteoroid") and [UV-light at entry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ozone_layer "Ozone layer"). It has a composition of primarily [nitrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen "Nitrogen") and [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen"). [Water vapor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vapor "Water vapor") is widely present in the atmosphere, [forming clouds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cloud#Formation "Cloud") that cover most of the planet. The water vapor acts as a [greenhouse gas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas "Greenhouse gas") and, together with other greenhouse gases in the atmosphere, particularly [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide") (CO 2), creates the conditions for both liquid surface water and water vapor to persist via the capturing of [energy from the Sun's light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_irradiance "Solar irradiance"). This process maintains the current average surface temperature of 14.76¬∞C (58.57¬∞F), at which water is liquid under normal atmospheric pressure. Differences in the amount of captured energy between geographic regions (as with the [equatorial region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equatorial_region "Equatorial region") receiving more sunlight than the polar regions) drive [atmospheric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation "Atmospheric circulation") and [ocean currents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_current "Ocean current"), producing a global [climate system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_system "Climate system") with different [climate regions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_region "Climate region"), and a range of weather phenomena such as [precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation"), allowing components such as [carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_cycle "Carbon cycle") and [nitrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle "Nitrogen cycle") to [cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle "Biogeochemical cycle").

Earth is [rounded](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium "Hydrostatic equilibrium") into [an ellipsoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_ellipsoid "Earth ellipsoid") with [a circumference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_circumference "Earth's circumference") of about 40,000 kilometres (25,000 miles). It is the [densest planet in the Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_size "List of Solar System objects by size"). Of the four [rocky planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rocky_planets "Rocky planets"), it is the largest and most massive. Earth is about eight [light-minutes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light-minute "Light-minute") (1 [AU](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_Unit "Astronomical Unit")) away from the Sun and [orbits it](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit "Earth's orbit"), taking a year (about 365.25 days) to complete one revolution. [Earth rotates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation "Earth's rotation") around its own axis in slightly less than a day (in about 23 hours and 56 minutes). [Earth's axis of rotation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#Axial_tilt_and_seasons) is tilted with respect to the perpendicular to its orbital plane around the Sun, producing seasons. Earth is [orbited](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbit "Orbit") by one permanent [natural satellite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_satellite "Natural satellite"), the [Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon "Moon"), which orbits Earth at 384,400 km (238,900 mi)‚Äî1.28 light seconds‚Äîand is roughly a quarter as wide as Earth. The Moon's gravity helps stabilize Earth's axis, causes [tides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tide "Tide") and [gradually slows Earth's rotation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_acceleration "Tidal acceleration"). Likewise Earth's gravitational pull has already made the Moon's rotation [tidally locked](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidally_locked "Tidally locked"), keeping the same [near side](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near_side_of_the_Moon "Near side of the Moon") facing Earth.

Earth, like most other bodies in the Solar System, [formed about 4.5 billion years ago](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_Earth "Age of Earth") from gas and dust in the [early Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System "Formation and evolution of the Solar System"). During the first billion years of [Earth's history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_history "Earth's history"), the ocean formed and then [life developed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis "Abiogenesis") within it. Life spread globally and has been altering Earth's atmosphere and surface, leading to the [Great Oxidation Event](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Oxidation_Event "Great Oxidation Event") two billion years ago. [Humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human "Human") emerged 300,000 years ago in Africa and have spread across every continent on Earth. Humans depend on Earth's [biosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biosphere "Biosphere") and natural resources for their survival, but have [increasingly impacted the planet's environment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment "Human impact on the environment"). Humanity's current impact on Earth's climate and biosphere is unsustainable, threatening the livelihood of humans and many other forms of life, and [causing widespread extinctions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holocene_extinction "Holocene extinction").

Etymology
---------

The [Modern English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_English "Modern English") word _Earth_ developed, via [Middle English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_English "Middle English"), from an [Old English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_English "Old English") noun most often spelled _[eor√∞e](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/eor%C3%B0e#Old\_English "wikt:eor√∞e")_.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-oedearth-28) It has cognates in every [Germanic language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germanic_language "Germanic language"), from which [Proto-Germanic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Germanic "Proto-Germanic")*_er√æ≈ç_ has been reconstructed. In its earliest attestation, the word _\_eor√∞e\__ was used to translate the many senses of Latin _[terra](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/terra#Latin "wikt:terra")_ and Greek _[gƒì](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B3%E1%BF%86#Ancient\_Greek "wikt:Œ≥·øÜ")_: the ground, its soil, dry land, the human world, the surface of the world (including the sea), and the globe itself. As with Roman [Terra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terra_(goddess) "Terra (goddess)") (or Tellus) and Greek [Gaia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaia "Gaia"), Earth may have been a [personified goddess](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_goddess "Earth goddess") in [Germanic paganism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germanic_paganism "Germanic paganism"): late [Norse mythology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norse_mythology "Norse mythology") included [J√∂r√∞](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J%C3%B6r%C3%B0 "J√∂r√∞") ('Earth'), a giantess often given as the mother of [Thor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thor "Thor").[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-29)

Historically, _Earth_ has been written in lowercase. During the [Early Middle English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Middle_English "Early Middle English") period, its [definite sense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Definite_article "Definite article") as "the globe" began being expressed using the phrase _the earth_. By the period of [Early Modern English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Modern_English "Early Modern English"), [capitalization of nouns began to prevail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capitalization_in_English#History_of_English_capitalization "Capitalization in English"), and _the earth_ was also written _the Earth_, particularly when referenced along with other heavenly bodies. More recently, the name is sometimes simply given as _Earth_, by analogy with the names of the [other planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System"), though _earth_ and forms with _the earth_ remain common.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-oedearth-28)[House styles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Style_guide "Style guide") now vary: [Oxford spelling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxford_spelling "Oxford spelling") recognizes the lowercase form as the more common, with the capitalized form an acceptable variant. Another convention capitalizes _Earth_ when appearing as a name, such as a description of the "Earth's atmosphere", but employs the lowercase when it is preceded by _the_, such as "the atmosphere of the earth". It almost always appears in lowercase in colloquial expressions such as "what on earth are you doing?"[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-30)

The name _Terra_[_TERR-…ô_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key") is occasionally used in scientific writing; it also sees use in science fiction to distinguish humanity's inhabited planet from others,[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-31) while in poetry _Tellus_[_TELL-…ôs_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key") has been used to denote personification of the Earth.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-32)_Terra_ is also the name of the planet in some [Romance languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romance_languages "Romance languages"), languages that evolved from Latin, like Italian and Portuguese, while in other Romance languages the word gave rise to names with slightly altered spellings, like the Spanish _Tierra_ and the French _Terre_. The Latinate form _Gaea_ ( [_DJEE-…ô_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key")) of the Greek poetic name _[Gaia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaia "Gaia")_ ([[…°√¢iÃØ.a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/Greek "Help:IPA/Greek") or [[…°√¢j.ja]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/Greek "Help:IPA/Greek")) is rare, though the alternative spelling _Gaia_ has become common due to the [Gaia hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis "Gaia hypothesis"), in which case its pronunciation is [_GYE-…ô_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key") rather than the more traditional English [_GAY-…ô_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key").[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-33)

There are a number of adjectives for the planet Earth. The word _earthly_ is derived from _Earth_. From the Latin _Terra_ comes _terran_[_TERR-…ôn_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key"),[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-34)_terrestrial_[_t…ôrr-EHST-ree-…ôl_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key"),[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-35) and (via French) _terrene_[_t…ô-REEN_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key"),[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-36) and from the Latin _Tellus_ comes _tellurian_[_teh-LUURR-ee-…ôn_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key")[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-37) and _telluric_.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-38)

Natural history
---------------

### Formation

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/The_Mysterious_Case_of_the_Disappearing_Dust.jpg/330px-The_Mysterious_Case_of_the_Disappearing_Dust.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_Mysterious_Case_of_the_Disappearing_Dust.jpg)

A 2012 artistic impression of the early [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System")'s [protoplanetary disk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk "Protoplanetary disk") from which Earth and other Solar System bodies were formed

The oldest material found in the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System") is dated to 4.5682+0.0002

‚àí0.0004[Ga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gigaannum "Gigaannum") (billion years) ago.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-bouvier_wadhwa2010-39) By 4.54¬±0.04 Ga the primordial Earth had formed.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-age_earth1-40) The bodies in [the Solar System formed and evolved](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System "Formation and evolution of the Solar System") with the Sun. In theory, a [solar nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_nebula "Solar nebula") partitions a volume out of a [molecular cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_cloud "Molecular cloud") by gravitational collapse, which begins to spin and flatten into a [circumstellar disk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circumstellar_disk "Circumstellar disk"), and then the planets grow out of that disk with the Sun. A nebula contains gas, ice grains, and [dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_dust "Cosmic dust") (including [primordial nuclides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primordial_nuclide "Primordial nuclide")). According to [nebular theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nebular_theory "Nebular theory"), [planetesimals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetesimal "Planetesimal") formed by [accretion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics) "Accretion (astrophysics)"), with the primordial Earth being estimated as likely taking anywhere from 70 to 100 million years to form.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-41)

Estimates of the age of the Moon range from 4.5 Ga to significantly younger.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-42) A [leading hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giant_impact_hypothesis "Giant impact hypothesis") is that it was formed by accretion from material loosed from Earth after a [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars")-sized object with about 10% of Earth's mass, named [Theia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theia_(planet) "Theia (planet)"), collided with Earth.[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-reilly20091022-43) It hit Earth with a glancing blow and some of its mass merged with Earth.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-canup_asphaug2001b-44)[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-45) Between approximately 4.0 and 3.8 Ga, numerous [asteroid impacts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_event "Impact event") during the [Late Heavy Bombardment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment "Late Heavy Bombardment") caused significant changes to the greater surface environment of the Moon and, by inference, to that of Earth.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-46)

### After formation

[Earth's atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth "Atmosphere of Earth") and oceans were formed by [volcanic activity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanism "Volcanism") and [outgassing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outgassing "Outgassing").[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-47) Water vapor from these sources [condensed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth "Origin of water on Earth") into the oceans, augmented by water and ice from asteroids, [protoplanets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protoplanet "Protoplanet"), and [comets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comet "Comet").[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-watersource-48) Sufficient water to fill the oceans may have been on Earth since it formed.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-49) In this model, atmospheric [greenhouse gases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas "Greenhouse gas") kept the oceans from freezing when the newly forming Sun [had only 70%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faint_young_Sun_paradox "Faint young Sun paradox") of its [current luminosity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_luminosity "Solar luminosity").[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-asp2002-50) By 3.5 Ga, [Earth's magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field "Earth's magnetic field") was established, which helped prevent the atmosphere from being stripped away by the [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind").[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-physorg20100304-51)

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/NASA-EarlyEarth-PaleOrangeDot-20190802.jpg/500px-NASA-EarlyEarth-PaleOrangeDot-20190802.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NASA-EarlyEarth-PaleOrangeDot-20190802.jpg)

_Pale orange dot_, an artist's impression of [Early Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Earth "Early Earth"), featuring its tinted orange [methane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methane "Methane")-rich [early atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prebiotic_atmosphere "Prebiotic atmosphere")[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Trainer_Pavlov_DeWitt_Jimenez_pp._18035%E2%80%9318042-52)

As the molten outer layer of Earth cooled it [formed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phase_transition "Phase transition") the first solid [crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_crust "Earth's crust"), which is thought to have been [mafic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mafic "Mafic") in composition. The first [continental crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continental_crust "Continental crust"), which was more [felsic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felsic "Felsic") in composition, formed by the partial melting of this mafic crust.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-comp-53) The presence of grains of the [mineral zircon of Hadean age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadean_zircon "Hadean zircon") in [Eoarchean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eoarchean "Eoarchean")[sedimentary rocks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sedimentary_rock "Sedimentary rock") suggests that at least some felsic crust existed as early as 4.4 Ga, only 140[Ma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Megaannum "Megaannum") after Earth's formation.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-science310_5756_1947-54) There are two main models of how this initial small volume of continental crust evolved to reach its current abundance:[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-williams_santosh2004-55) (1) a relatively steady growth up to the present day,[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-science164_1229-56) which is supported by the radiometric dating of continental crust globally and (2) an initial rapid growth in the volume of continental crust during the [Archean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archean "Archean"), forming the bulk of the continental crust that now exists,[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ajes38_613-57)[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-tp322_19-58) which is supported by isotopic evidence from [hafnium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hafnium "Hafnium") in [zircons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zircon "Zircon") and [neodymium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neodymium "Neodymium") in sedimentary rocks. The two models and the data that support them can be reconciled by large-scale [recycling of the continental crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crustal_recycling "Crustal recycling"), particularly during the early stages of Earth's history.[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Dhuime_etal_2018-59)

New continental crust forms as a result of [plate tectonics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plate_tectonics "Plate tectonics"), a process ultimately driven by the continuous loss of heat from Earth's interior. Over [the period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geologic_time_scale "Geologic time scale") of hundreds of millions of years, tectonic forces have caused areas of continental crust to group together to form [supercontinents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supercontinent "Supercontinent") that have subsequently broken apart. At approximately 750 Ma, one of the earliest known supercontinents, [Rodinia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rodinia "Rodinia"), began to break apart. The continents later recombined to form [Pannotia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pannotia "Pannotia") at 600‚Äì540 Ma, then finally [Pangaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pangaea "Pangaea"), which also began to break apart at 180 Ma.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-bradley_2011-60)

The most recent pattern of [ice ages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_age "Ice age") began about 40 Ma,[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-61) and then intensified during the [Pleistocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pleistocene "Pleistocene") about 3 Ma.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-62)[High-](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_latitude "High latitude") and [middle-latitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_latitude "Middle latitude") regions have since undergone repeated cycles of glaciation and thaw, repeating about every 21,000, 41,000 and 100,000 years.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-psc-63) The [Last Glacial Period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_Glacial_Period "Last Glacial Period"), colloquially called the "last ice age", covered large parts of the continents, to the middle latitudes, in ice and ended about 11,700 years ago.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-64)

### Origin of life and evolution

[Chemical reactions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_reaction "Chemical reaction") led to the first self-replicating molecules about four billion years ago. A half billion years later, the [last common ancestor of all current life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor") arose.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-sa282_6_90-65) The evolution of [photosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosynthesis "Photosynthesis") allowed the Sun's energy to be harvested directly by life forms. The resultant [molecular oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_oxygen "Molecular oxygen") (O 2) accumulated in the atmosphere and due to interaction with ultraviolet solar radiation, formed a protective [ozone layer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ozone_layer "Ozone layer") (O 3) in the upper atmosphere.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-NYT-20131003-66) The incorporation of smaller cells within larger ones resulted in the [development of complex cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endosymbiotic_theory "Endosymbiotic theory") called [eukaryotes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryote "Eukaryote").[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-jas22_3_225-67) True multicellular organisms formed as cells within [colonies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colony_(biology) "Colony (biology)") became increasingly specialized. Aided by the absorption of harmful [ultraviolet radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation "Ultraviolet radiation") by the ozone layer, life colonized Earth's surface.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-burton20021129-68) Among the earliest [fossil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil "Fossil") evidence for life is [microbial mat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microbial_mat "Microbial mat") fossils found in 3.48 billion-year-old [sandstone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sandstone "Sandstone") in [Western Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Australia "Western Australia"),[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-69)[biogenic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biogenic_substance "Biogenic substance")[graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite") found in 3.7 billion-year-old [metasedimentary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metasediment "Metasediment") rocks in [Western Greenland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Greenland "Western Greenland"),[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-70) and remains of [biotic material](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biotic_material "Biotic material") found in 4.1 billion-year-old rocks in Western Australia.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-71)[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-72) The [earliest direct evidence of life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms "Earliest known life forms") on Earth is contained in 3.45 billion-year-old [Australian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia_(continent) "Australia (continent)") rocks showing fossils of [microorganisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microorganism "Microorganism").[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-73)[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-74)

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Archean.png/330px-Archean.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Archean.png)

An artist's impression of the [Archean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archean "Archean"), the [eon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geologic_time_scale#Divisions_of_geologic_time "Geologic time scale") after Earth's formation, featuring round [stromatolites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stromatolite "Stromatolite"), which are early oxygen-producing forms of life from billions of years ago. After the [Late Heavy Bombardment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment "Late Heavy Bombardment"), [Earth's crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_crust "Earth's crust") had cooled, its water-rich barren [surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_surface "Planetary surface") is marked by [continents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continent "Continent") and [volcanoes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcano "Volcano"), with the Moon still orbiting Earth half as far as it is today, appearing 2.8 times larger and producing strong [tides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tide "Tide").[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Lunar_and_Planetary_Institute-75)

During the [Neoproterozoic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neoproterozoic "Neoproterozoic"), 1000 to 539 Ma, much of Earth might have been covered in ice. This hypothesis has been termed "[Snowball Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snowball_Earth "Snowball Earth")", and it is of particular interest because it preceded the [Cambrian explosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cambrian_explosion "Cambrian explosion"), when multicellular life forms significantly increased in complexity.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-76)[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-77) Following the Cambrian explosion, 535 Ma, there have been at least five major [mass extinctions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extinction_event "Extinction event") and many minor ones.[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Stanley_2016-78) Apart from the proposed current [Holocene extinction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holocene_extinction "Holocene extinction") event, the [most recent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event "Cretaceous‚ÄìPaleogene extinction event") was 66 Ma, when [an asteroid impact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chicxulub_impactor "Chicxulub impactor") triggered the extinction of non-avian dinosaurs and other large reptiles, but largely spared small animals such as insects, [mammals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mammal "Mammal"), lizards and birds. Mammalian life has diversified over the past 66 Mys, and several million years ago, an African [ape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ape "Ape") species gained the ability to stand upright.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-gould1994-79)[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-80) This facilitated tool use and encouraged communication that provided the nutrition and stimulation needed for a larger brain, which led to the [evolution of humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution "Human evolution"). The [development of agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_agriculture "History of agriculture"), and then [civilization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_ancient_civilizations "List of ancient civilizations"), led to humans having an [influence on Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment "Human impact on the environment") and the nature and quantity of other life forms that continues to this day.[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-bgsa119_1_140-81)

### Future

[![Image 7: A dark gray and red sphere representing the Earth lies against a black background to the right of an orange circular object representing the Sun](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Red_Giant_Earth_warm.jpg/330px-Red_Giant_Earth_warm.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Red_Giant_Earth_warm.jpg)

Conjectured illustration of the scorched Earth after the [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun") has entered the [red giant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_giant "Red giant") phase, about 5‚Äì7 billion years in the future

Earth's expected long-term future is tied to that of the Sun. Over the next 1.1 billion years, solar luminosity will increase by 10%, and over the next 3.5 billion years by 40%.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-sun_future-82) Earth's increasing surface temperature will accelerate the [inorganic carbon cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbonate%E2%80%93silicate_cycle "Carbonate‚Äìsilicate cycle"), possibly reducing CO 2 concentration to levels lethally low for current plants (10[ppm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parts_per_million "Parts per million") for [C 4 photosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation "C4 carbon fixation")) in approximately 100‚Äì900 million years.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-britt2000-83)[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-pnas1_24_9576-84) A lack of vegetation would result in the loss of oxygen in the atmosphere, making current animal life impossible.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ward_brownlee2002-85) Due to the increased luminosity, Earth's mean temperature may reach 100¬∞C (212¬∞F) in 1.5 billion years, and all ocean water will evaporate and be lost to space, which may trigger a [runaway greenhouse effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Runaway_greenhouse_effect "Runaway greenhouse effect"), within an estimated 1.6 to 3 billion years.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Mello-2020-86) Even if the Sun were stable and eternal, a significant fraction of the water in the modern oceans would descend into the [mantle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mantle_(geology) "Mantle (geology)"), due to reduced steam venting from mid-ocean ridges as the core of the Earth slowly cools.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Mello-2020-86)[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-hess5_4_569-87)

The Sun will [evolve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_evolution "Stellar evolution") to become a [red giant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_giant "Red giant") in about 5 billion years. Models predict that the Sun will expand to roughly 1[AU](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_unit "Astronomical unit") (150 million km; 93 million mi), about 250 times its present radius.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-sun_future-82)[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-sun_future_schroder-88) Earth's fate is less clear. As a red giant, the Sun will lose roughly 30% of its mass, so, without tidal effects, Earth will move to an orbit 1.7 AU (250 million km; 160 million mi) from the Sun when the star reaches its maximum radius, otherwise, with tidal effects, it may enter the Sun's atmosphere and be vaporized, with the heavier elements sinking to the core of the dying sun.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-sun_future-82)

Physical characteristics
------------------------

### Size and shape

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg/330px-Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg)

Earth's western hemisphere showing topography relative to Earth's center instead of to [mean sea level](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mean_sea_level "Mean sea level"), as in common topographic maps

[Earth has a rounded shape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Figure_of_the_Earth "Figure of the Earth"), through [hydrostatic equilibrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium "Hydrostatic equilibrium"),[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Horner_2021-89) with an average diameter of 12,742 kilometres (7,918 mi), making it the [fifth largest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_size "List of Solar System objects by size")[planetary sized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#Planetary-mass_object "Planet") and largest [terrestrial object](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrestrial_planet "Terrestrial planet") of the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System").[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-90)

Due to [Earth's rotation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation "Earth's rotation") it has the shape of an [ellipsoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_ellipsoid "Earth ellipsoid"), [bulging at its equator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equatorial_bulge "Equatorial bulge"); its diameter is 43 kilometres (27 mi) longer there than at its [poles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geographical_pole "Geographical pole").[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ngdc2006-91)[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-milbert_smith96-92) Earth's shape also has local [topographic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topography "Topography") variations; the largest local variations, like the [Mariana Trench](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mariana_Trench "Mariana Trench") (10,925 metres or 35,843 feet below local sea level),[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-93) shortens Earth's average radius by 0.17% and [Mount Everest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Everest "Mount Everest") (8,848 metres or 29,029 feet above local sea level) lengthens it by 0.14%.[[n 6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-95)[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-96) Since Earth's surface is farthest out from its [center of mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Center_of_mass "Center of mass") at its equatorial bulge, the summit of the volcano [Chimborazo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimborazo "Chimborazo") in Ecuador (6,384.4 km or 3,967.1 mi) is its farthest point out.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ps20_5_16-97)[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-98) Parallel to the rigid land topography [the ocean exhibits a more dynamic topography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_surface_topography "Ocean surface topography").[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-99)

To measure the local variation of Earth's topography, [geodesy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodesy "Geodesy") employs an idealized Earth producing a [geoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoid "Geoid") shape. Such a shape is gained if the ocean is idealized, covering Earth completely and without any perturbations such as tides and winds. The result is a smooth but irregular geoid surface, providing a mean sea level as a reference level for topographic measurements.[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-100)

### Surface

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Global_View_of_the_Arctic_and_Antarctic.jpg/250px-Global_View_of_the_Arctic_and_Antarctic.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Global_View_of_the_Arctic_and_Antarctic.jpg)

A [composite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compositing "Compositing") image of Earth, with its different types of surface discernible: Earth's surface dominating Ocean (blue), Africa with lush (green) to dry (brown) land and Earth's polar ice in the form of [Antarctic sea ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctic_sea_ice "Antarctic sea ice") (grey) covering the [Antarctic or Southern Ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Ocean "Southern Ocean") and the [Antarctic ice sheet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet "Antarctic ice sheet") (white) covering [Antarctica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica "Antarctica").

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/AYool_topography_15min.png/330px-AYool_topography_15min.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:AYool_topography_15min.png)

[Relief](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrain "Terrain") of [Earth's crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_crust "Earth's crust")

Earth's surface is the boundary between the atmosphere and the solid Earth and oceans. Defined in this way, it has an area of about 510 million km 2 (197 million sq mi).[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Pidwirny_2006_8-15) Earth can be divided into two [hemispheres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemispheres_of_Earth "Hemispheres of Earth"): by [latitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latitude "Latitude") into the polar [Northern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere "Northern Hemisphere") and [Southern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere "Southern Hemisphere") hemispheres; or by [longitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Longitude "Longitude") into the continental [Eastern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Hemisphere "Eastern Hemisphere") and [Western](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Hemisphere "Western Hemisphere") hemispheres.

Most of Earth's surface is ocean water: 70.8% or 361 million km 2 (139 million sq mi).[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Pidwirny_2006_8-15) This vast pool of salty water is often called the _world ocean_,[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Janin_Mandia_2012_p._20-101)[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Ro_2020-102) and makes Earth with its dynamic [hydrosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrosphere "Hydrosphere") a water world[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Smith_2021-103)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-National_Geographic_Society_2022-104) or [ocean world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_world "Ocean world").[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Lunine_2017_pp._123%E2%80%93130-105)[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Ocean_Worlds-106) Indeed, in Earth's early history the ocean may have covered Earth completely.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Voosen_p.-107) The world ocean is commonly divided into the Pacific Ocean, Atlantic Ocean, Indian Ocean, [Antarctic or Southern Ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Ocean "Southern Ocean"), and Arctic Ocean, from largest to smallest. The ocean covers [Earth's oceanic crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceanic_crust "Oceanic crust"), with the [shelf seas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shelf_sea "Shelf sea") covering the [shelves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continental_shelf "Continental shelf") of the [continental crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continental_crust "Continental crust") to a lesser extent. The oceanic crust forms large [oceanic basins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceanic_basin "Oceanic basin") with features like [abyssal plains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abyssal_plain "Abyssal plain"), [seamounts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seamount "Seamount"), [submarine volcanoes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Submarine_volcano "Submarine volcano"),[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ngdc2006-91)[oceanic trenches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceanic_trench "Oceanic trench"), [submarine canyons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Submarine_canyon "Submarine canyon"), [oceanic plateaus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceanic_plateau "Oceanic plateau"), and a globe-spanning [mid-ocean ridge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge "Mid-ocean ridge") system.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-108) At Earth's [polar regions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_regions_of_Earth "Polar regions of Earth"), the [ocean surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_surface "Ocean surface") is covered by seasonally variable amounts of [sea ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_ice "Sea ice") that often connects with polar land, [permafrost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permafrost "Permafrost") and [ice sheets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_sheet "Ice sheet"), forming [polar ice caps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_ice_cap "Polar ice cap").

Earth's land covers 29.2%, or 149 million km 2 (58 million sq mi) of Earth's surface. The land surface includes many islands around the globe, but most of the land surface is taken by the four continental [landmasses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landmass "Landmass"), which are (in descending order): [Africa-Eurasia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afro-Eurasia "Afro-Eurasia"), [America (landmass)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas "Americas"), [Antarctica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica "Antarctica"), and [Australia (landmass)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mainland_Australia "Mainland Australia").[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-DunnMitchell2016-109)[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Dempsey_2013-110)[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-McColl-111) These landmasses are further broken down and grouped into the [continents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continent "Continent"). The [terrain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrain "Terrain") of the land surface varies greatly and consists of mountains, [deserts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desert "Desert"), [plains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plain "Plain"), [plateaus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plateau "Plateau"), and other [landforms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landform "Landform"). The elevation of the land surface varies from a low point of ‚àí418 m (‚àí1,371 ft) at the [Dead Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dead_Sea "Dead Sea"), to a maximum altitude of 8,848 m (29,029 ft) at the top of [Mount Everest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Everest "Mount Everest"). The mean height of land above sea level is about 797 m (2,615 ft).[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-112)

Land can be [covered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_cover "Land cover") by [surface water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_water "Surface water"), snow, ice, artificial structures or vegetation. Most of Earth's land hosts vegetation,[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Carlowicz_Simmon_2019-113) but considerable amounts of land are [ice sheets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_sheet "Ice sheet") (10%,[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-National_Geographic_Society_2006-114) not including the equally large area of land under [permafrost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permafrost "Permafrost"))[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Obu_2021_p.-115) or [deserts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desert "Desert") (33%).[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Cain_2010-116)

The [pedosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pedosphere "Pedosphere") is the outermost layer of Earth's land surface and is composed of soil and subject to [soil formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_formation "Soil formation") processes. Soil is crucial for land to be arable. Earth's total [arable land](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arable_land "Arable land") is 10.7% of the land surface, with 1.3% being permanent cropland.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-117)[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-118) Earth has an estimated 16.7 million km 2 (6.4 million sq mi) of cropland and 33.5 million km 2 (12.9 million sq mi) of pastureland.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Hooke2012-119)

The land surface and the [ocean floor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_floor "Ocean floor") form the top of [Earth's crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_crust "Earth's crust"), which together with parts of the [upper mantle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_mantle_(Earth) "Upper mantle (Earth)") form [Earth's lithosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithosphere#Earth's_lithosphere "Lithosphere"). Earth's crust may be divided into [oceanic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceanic_crust "Oceanic crust") and [continental](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continental_crust "Continental crust") crust. Beneath the ocean-floor sediments, the oceanic crust is predominantly [basaltic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basalt "Basalt"), while the continental crust may include lower density materials such as [granite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Granite "Granite"), sediments and metamorphic rocks.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-layers_earth-120) Nearly 75% of the continental surfaces are covered by sedimentary rocks, although they form about 5% of the mass of the crust.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-jessey-121)

Earth's surface [topography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topography "Topography") comprises both the [topography of the ocean surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_surface_topography "Ocean surface topography"), and the [shape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypsometry "Hypsometry") of Earth's land surface. The submarine terrain of the ocean floor has an average [bathymetric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bathymetric "Bathymetric") depth of 4 km, and is as varied as the terrain above sea level. Earth's surface is continually being shaped by internal [plate tectonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plate_tectonic "Plate tectonic") processes including [earthquakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earthquakes "Earthquakes") and [volcanism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanism "Volcanism"); by [weathering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weathering "Weathering") and [erosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erosion "Erosion") driven by ice, water, wind and temperature; and by [biological processes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_processes "Biological processes") including the growth and decomposition of [biomass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biomass "Biomass") into [soil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil "Soil").[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-kring-122)[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-123)

### Tectonic plates

[![Image 11: Shows the extent and boundaries of tectonic plates, with superimposed outlines of the continents they support](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Tectonic_plates_%28empty%29.svg/250px-Tectonic_plates_%28empty%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tectonic_plates_(empty).svg)

[Earth's major plates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_tectonic_plates "List of tectonic plates"), which are:[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-brown_wohletz2005-124)

*   [Pacific Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Plate "Pacific Plate")
*   [African Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_Plate "African Plate")[[n 7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-jaes41_3_379-125)
*   [North American Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_American_Plate "North American Plate")
*   [Eurasian Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasian_Plate "Eurasian Plate")
*   [Antarctic Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctic_Plate "Antarctic Plate")
*   [Indo-Australian Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate "Indo-Australian Plate")
*   [South American Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_American_Plate "South American Plate")

Earth's mechanically rigid outer layer of [Earth's crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_crust "Earth's crust") and [upper mantle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_mantle_(Earth) "Upper mantle (Earth)"), the [lithosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithosphere "Lithosphere"), is divided into [tectonic plates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_tectonic_plates "List of tectonic plates"). These plates are rigid segments that move relative to each other at one of three boundaries types: at [convergent boundaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_boundary "Convergent boundary"), two plates come together; at [divergent boundaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divergent_boundary "Divergent boundary"), two plates are pulled apart; and at [transform boundaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transform_fault "Transform fault"), two plates slide past one another laterally. Along these plate boundaries, earthquakes, [volcanic activity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanism "Volcanism"), [mountain-building](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orogeny "Orogeny"), and [oceanic trench](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceanic_trench "Oceanic trench") formation can occur.[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-kious_tilling1999-126) The tectonic plates ride on top of the [asthenosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asthenosphere "Asthenosphere"), the solid but less-viscous part of the upper mantle that can flow and move along with the plates.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-seligman2008-127)

As the tectonic plates migrate, oceanic crust is [subducted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subduction "Subduction") under the leading edges of the plates at convergent boundaries. At the same time, the upwelling of mantle material at divergent boundaries creates mid-ocean ridges. The combination of these processes recycles the oceanic crust back into the mantle. Due to this recycling, most of the ocean floor is less than 100 Ma old. The oldest oceanic crust is located in the Western Pacific and is estimated to be 200 Ma old.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-duennebier1999-128)[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-noaa20070307-129) By comparison, the oldest dated continental crust is 4,030 Ma,[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-cmp134_3-130) although zircons have been found preserved as clasts within Eoarchean sedimentary rocks that give ages up to 4,400 Ma, indicating that at least some continental crust existed at that time.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-science310_5756_1947-54)

The seven major plates are the [Pacific](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Plate "Pacific Plate"), [North American](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_American_Plate "North American Plate"), [Eurasian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasian_Plate "Eurasian Plate"), [African](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_Plate "African Plate"), [Antarctic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctic_Plate "Antarctic Plate"), [Indo-Australian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate "Indo-Australian Plate"), and [South American](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_American_Plate "South American Plate"). Other notable plates include the [Arabian Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabian_Plate "Arabian Plate"), the [Caribbean Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caribbean_Plate "Caribbean Plate"), the [Nazca Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazca_Plate "Nazca Plate") off the west coast of South America and the [Scotia Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scotia_Plate "Scotia Plate") in the southern Atlantic Ocean. The Australian Plate fused with the Indian Plate between 50 and 55 Ma. The fastest-moving plates are the oceanic plates, with the [Cocos Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cocos_Plate "Cocos Plate") advancing at a rate of 75 mm/a (3.0 in/year)[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-podp2000-131) and the Pacific Plate moving 52‚Äì69 mm/a (2.0‚Äì2.7 in/year). At the other extreme, the slowest-moving plate is the South American Plate, progressing at a typical rate of 10.6 mm/a (0.42 in/year).[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Argus_etal_2011-132)

### Internal structure

Geologic layers of Earth[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-pnas76_9_4192-133)[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Earth_cutaway_schematic-en.svg/250px-Earth_cutaway_schematic-en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Earth_cutaway_schematic-en.svg)

Illustration of Earth's cutaway, not to scale
