Earth's interior, like that of the other terrestrial planets, is divided into layers by their [chemical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical "Chemical") or physical ([rheological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rheology "Rheology")) properties. The outer layer is a chemically distinct [silicate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicate_minerals "Silicate minerals") solid crust, which is underlain by a highly [viscous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viscous "Viscous") solid mantle. The crust is separated from the mantle by the [Mohoroviƒçiƒá discontinuity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohorovi%C4%8Di%C4%87_discontinuity "Mohoroviƒçiƒá discontinuity").[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-GeolSoc-137) The thickness of the crust varies from about 6 kilometres (3.7 mi) under the oceans to 30‚Äì50 km (19‚Äì31 mi) for the continents. The crust and the cold, rigid, top of the [upper mantle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_mantle "Upper mantle") are collectively known as the lithosphere, which is divided into independently moving tectonic plates.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-138)

Beneath the lithosphere is the [asthenosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asthenosphere "Asthenosphere"), a relatively low-viscosity layer on which the lithosphere rides. Important changes in crystal structure within the mantle occur at 410 and 660 km (250 and 410 mi) below the surface, spanning a [transition zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transition_zone_(Earth) "Transition zone (Earth)") that separates the upper and lower mantle. Beneath the mantle, an extremely low viscosity liquid [outer core](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_core "Outer core") lies above a solid [inner core](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_inner_core "Earth's inner core").[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-tanimoto_ahrens1995-139) Earth's inner core may be rotating at a slightly higher [angular velocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_velocity "Angular velocity") than the remainder of the planet, advancing by 0.1‚Äì0.5¬∞ per year, although both somewhat higher and much lower rates have also been proposed.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Deuss_2014-140) The radius of the inner core is about one-fifth of that of Earth. The density increases with depth. Among the Solar System's planetary-sized objects, Earth is the [object with the highest density](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_size "List of Solar System objects by size").

### Chemical composition

[Earth's mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_mass "Earth mass") is approximately 5.97√ó10 24[kg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilogram "Kilogram") (5.970[Yg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yottagram "Yottagram")). It is composed mostly of iron (32.1% [by mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_fraction_(chemistry) "Mass fraction (chemistry)")), [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") (30.1%), [silicon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon "Silicon") (15.1%), [magnesium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnesium "Magnesium") (13.9%), [sulfur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfur "Sulfur") (2.9%), [nickel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nickel "Nickel") (1.8%), [calcium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calcium "Calcium") (1.5%), and [aluminium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aluminium "Aluminium") (1.4%), with the remaining 1.2% consisting of trace amounts of other elements. Due to [gravitational separation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_differentiation#Gravitational_separation "Planetary differentiation"), the core is primarily composed of the denser elements: iron (88.8%), with smaller amounts of nickel (5.8%), sulfur (4.5%), and less than 1% trace elements.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-pnas71_12_6973-141)[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-comp-53) The most common rock constituents of the crust are [oxides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxide "Oxide"). Over 99% of the [crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_crust "Earth's crust") is composed of various oxides of eleven elements, principally oxides containing silicon (the [silicate minerals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicate_mineral "Silicate mineral")), aluminium, iron, calcium, magnesium, potassium, or sodium.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-brown_mussett1981-142)[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-pnas71_12_6973-141)

### Internal heat

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Earth_heat_flow.jpg/330px-Earth_heat_flow.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Earth_heat_flow.jpg)

A map of [heat flow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_flow "Heat flow") from Earth's interior to the surface of Earth's crust, mostly along the [oceanic ridges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceanic_ridge "Oceanic ridge")

The major contributors to Earth's internal heat are primordial heat (heat left over from Earth's formation) and radiogenic heat (heat produced by radioactive decay).[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Turcotte-143) The major heat-producing [isotopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotope "Isotope") within Earth are [potassium-40](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium-40 "Potassium-40"), [uranium-238](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uranium-238 "Uranium-238"), and [thorium-232](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thorium-232 "Thorium-232").[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-sanders20031210-144) At the center, the temperature may be up to 6,000¬∞C (10,830¬∞F),[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-145) and the pressure could reach 360[GPa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal_(unit) "Pascal (unit)") (52 million[psi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pound_per_square_inch "Pound per square inch")).[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ptrsl360_1795_1227-146) Because much of the heat is provided by radioactive decay, scientists postulate that early in Earth's history, before isotopes with short half-lives were depleted, Earth's heat production was much higher. At approximately 3[Gyr](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Billion_years "Billion years"), twice the present-day heat would have been produced, increasing the rates of [mantle convection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mantle_convection "Mantle convection") and plate tectonics, and allowing the production of uncommon [igneous rocks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Igneous_rock "Igneous rock") such as [komatiites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Komatiite "Komatiite") that are rarely formed today.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-T&S_137-147)[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-epsl121_1-148)

The mean heat loss from Earth is 87 mW/m 2, for a global heat loss of 4.42√ó10 13 W.[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-jg31_3_267-149) A portion of the core's thermal energy is transported toward the crust by [mantle plumes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mantle_plume "Mantle plume"), a form of convection consisting of upwellings of higher-temperature rock. These plumes can produce [hotspots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hotspot_(geology) "Hotspot (geology)") and [flood basalts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flood_basalt "Flood basalt").[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-science246_4926_103-150) More of the heat in Earth is lost through plate tectonics, by mantle upwelling associated with [mid-ocean ridges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge "Mid-ocean ridge"). The final major mode of heat loss is through conduction through the lithosphere, the majority of which occurs under the oceans.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-heat_loss-151)

### Gravitational field

The gravity of Earth is the [acceleration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acceleration "Acceleration") that is imparted to objects due to the distribution of mass within Earth. Near Earth's surface, [gravitational acceleration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_acceleration "Gravitational acceleration") is approximately 9.8 m/s 2 (32 ft/s 2). Local differences in topography, geology, and deeper tectonic structure cause local and broad regional differences in Earth's gravitational field, known as [gravity anomalies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity_anomaly "Gravity anomaly").[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-152)

### Magnetic field

[![Image 14: Diagram showing the magnetic field lines of Earth's magnetosphere. The lines are swept back in the anti-solar direction under the influence of the solar wind.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Magnetosphere_Levels-en.svg/250px-Magnetosphere_Levels-en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Magnetosphere_Levels-en.svg)

A schematic view of Earth's magnetosphere with [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind") flowing from left to right

The main part of Earth's magnetic field is generated in the core, the site of a [dynamo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamo_theory "Dynamo theory") process that converts the kinetic energy of thermally and compositionally driven convection into electrical and magnetic field energy. The field extends outwards from the core, through the mantle, and up to Earth's surface, where it is, approximately, a [dipole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dipole "Dipole"). The poles of the dipole are located close to Earth's geographic poles. At the equator of the magnetic field, the magnetic-field strength at the surface is 3.05√ó10‚àí5[T](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tesla_(unit) "Tesla (unit)"), with a [magnetic dipole moment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_dipole_moment "Magnetic dipole moment") of 7.79√ó10 22 Am 2 at epoch 2000, decreasing nearly 6% per century (although it still remains stronger than its long time average).[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-dipole-153) The convection movements in the core are chaotic; the magnetic poles drift and periodically change alignment. This causes [secular variation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geomagnetic_secular_variation "Geomagnetic secular variation") of the main field and [field reversals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal "Geomagnetic reversal") at irregular intervals averaging a few times every million years. The most recent reversal occurred approximately 700,000 years ago.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-fitzpatrick2006-154)[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-campbelwh-155)

The extent of Earth's magnetic field in space defines the [magnetosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetosphere "Magnetosphere"). Ions and electrons of the solar wind are deflected by the magnetosphere; solar wind pressure compresses the day-side of the magnetosphere, to about 10 Earth radii, and extends the night-side magnetosphere into a long tail.[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-156) Because the velocity of the solar wind is greater than the speed at which waves propagate through the solar wind, a supersonic [bow shock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bow_shock "Bow shock") precedes the day-side magnetosphere within the solar wind.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-157)[Charged particles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charged_particle "Charged particle") are contained within the magnetosphere; the plasmasphere is defined by low-energy particles that essentially follow magnetic field lines as Earth rotates.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-158)[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-159) The ring current is defined by medium-energy [particles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particle "Particle") that drift relative to the geomagnetic field, but with paths that are still dominated by the magnetic field,[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-BaumjohannTreumann1997-160) and the [Van Allen radiation belts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Van_Allen_radiation_belt "Van Allen radiation belt") are formed by high-energy particles whose motion is essentially random, but contained in the magnetosphere.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Britannica-161)[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Van_Allen-162) During [magnetic storms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_storm "Magnetic storm") and [substorms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Substorm "Substorm"), charged particles can be deflected from the outer magnetosphere and especially the magnetotail, directed along field lines into Earth's [ionosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionosphere "Ionosphere"), where atmospheric atoms can be excited and ionized, causing an [aurora](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurora "Aurora").[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-stern2005-163)

Orbit and rotation
------------------

### Rotation

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/EpicEarth-Globespin-tilt-23.4.gif/330px-EpicEarth-Globespin-tilt-23.4.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:EpicEarth-Globespin-tilt-23.4.gif)

Satellite [time lapse imagery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time-lapse_photography "Time-lapse photography") of Earth's rotation showing axis tilt

Earth's rotation period relative to the Sun‚Äîits mean solar day‚Äîis 86,400 seconds of mean solar time (86,400.0025 [SI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SI "SI") seconds).[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-aj136_5_1906-164) Because Earth's solar day is now slightly longer than it was during the 19th century due to [tidal deceleration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_acceleration "Tidal acceleration"), each day varies between 0 and 2 [ms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millisecond "Millisecond") longer than the mean solar day.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-USNO_TSD-165)[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-166)

Earth's rotation period relative to the [fixed stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fixed_star "Fixed star"), called its _stellar day_ by the [International Earth Rotation and Reference Systems Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Earth_Rotation_and_Reference_Systems_Service "International Earth Rotation and Reference Systems Service") (IERS), is 86,164.0989 seconds of mean solar time ([UT1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UT1 "UT1")), or 23 h 56 m 4.0989 s.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-IERS-4)[[n 10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Aoki-167) Earth's rotation period relative to the [precessing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precession_(astronomy) "Precession (astronomy)") or moving mean [March equinox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/March_equinox "March equinox") (when the Sun is at 90¬∞ on the equator), is 86,164.0905 seconds of mean solar time (UT1) (23 h 56 m 4.0905 s).[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-IERS-4) Thus the sidereal day is shorter than the stellar day by about 8.4 ms.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-seidelmann1992-168)

Apart from meteors within the atmosphere and low-orbiting satellites, the main apparent motion of celestial bodies in Earth's sky is to the west at a rate of 15¬∞/h = 15'/min. For bodies near the [celestial equator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_equator "Celestial equator"), this is equivalent to an apparent diameter of the Sun or the Moon every two minutes; from Earth's surface, the apparent sizes of the Sun and the Moon are approximately the same.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-zeilik1998-169)[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-angular-170)

### Orbit

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Seasons1.svg/330px-Seasons1.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Seasons1.svg)

Exaggerated illustration of Earth's elliptical orbit around the Sun, marking that the orbital extreme points ([apoapsis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apoapsis "Apoapsis") and [periapsis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Periapsis "Periapsis")) are not the same as the four seasonal extreme points, the [equinox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equinox "Equinox") and [solstice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solstice "Solstice")

Earth orbits the Sun, making Earth the third-closest planet to the Sun and part of the [inner Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inner_Solar_System "Inner Solar System"). Earth's average orbital distance is about 150 million km (93 million mi), which is the basis for the [astronomical unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_unit "Astronomical unit") (AU) and is equal to roughly 8.3 [light minutes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light_minute "Light minute") or 380 times [Earth's distance to the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_distance_(astronomy) "Lunar distance (astronomy)"). Earth orbits the Sun every 365.2564 mean [solar days](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_day "Solar day"), or one [sidereal year](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sidereal_year "Sidereal year"). With an apparent movement of the Sun in Earth's sky at a rate of about 1¬∞/day eastward, which is one apparent Sun or Moon diameter every 12 hours. Due to this motion, on average it takes 24 hours‚Äîa solar day‚Äîfor Earth to complete a full rotation about its axis so that the Sun returns to the [meridian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meridian_(astronomy) "Meridian (astronomy)").

The orbital speed of Earth averages about 29.78 km/s (107,200 km/h; 66,600 mph), which is fast enough to travel a distance equal to Earth's diameter, about 12,742 km (7,918 mi), in seven minutes, and the distance from Earth to the Moon, 384,400 km (238,900 mi), in about 3.5 hours.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-earth_fact_sheet-5)

The Moon and Earth orbit a common [barycenter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barycenter "Barycenter") every 27.32 days relative to the background stars. When combined with the Earth‚ÄìMoon system's common orbit around the Sun, the period of the [synodic month](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synodic_month "Synodic month"), from new moon to new moon, is 29.53 days. Viewed from the [celestial north pole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_pole "Celestial pole"), the motion of Earth, the Moon, and their axial rotations are all [counterclockwise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counterclockwise "Counterclockwise"). Viewed from a vantage point above the Sun and Earth's north poles, Earth orbits in a counterclockwise direction about the Sun. The orbital and axial planes are not precisely aligned: Earth's [axis is tilted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axial_tilt "Axial tilt") some 23.44 degrees from the perpendicular to the Earth‚ÄìSun plane (the [ecliptic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecliptic "Ecliptic")), and the Earth-Moon plane is tilted up to ¬±5.1 degrees against the Earth‚ÄìSun plane. Without this tilt, there would be an eclipse every two weeks, alternating between [lunar eclipses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_eclipse "Lunar eclipse") and [solar eclipses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_eclipse "Solar eclipse").[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-earth_fact_sheet-5)[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-moon_fact_sheet-171)

The [Hill sphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hill_sphere "Hill sphere"), or the [sphere of gravitational influence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphere_of_influence_(astrodynamics) "Sphere of influence (astrodynamics)"), of Earth is about 1.5 million km (930,000 mi) in radius.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-vazquez_etal2006-172)[[n 11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-hill_radius-173) This is the maximum distance at which Earth's gravitational influence is stronger than that of the more distant Sun and planets. Objects must orbit Earth within this radius, or they can become unbound by the gravitational perturbation of the Sun.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-vazquez_etal2006-172) Earth, along with the Solar System, is situated in the [Milky Way](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way "Milky Way") and orbits about 28,000[light-years](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light-year "Light-year") from its center. It is about 20 light-years above the [galactic plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_plane "Galactic plane") in the [Orion Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_Arm "Orion Arm").[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-nasa20051201-174)

### Axial tilt and seasons

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Axial_tilt_vs_tropical_and_polar_circles.svg/330px-Axial_tilt_vs_tropical_and_polar_circles.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Axial_tilt_vs_tropical_and_polar_circles.svg)

Earth's axial tilt causing different angles of seasonal illumination at different orbital positions around the Sun

The axial tilt of Earth is approximately 23.439281¬∞[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-IERS-4) with the axis of the plane of the [Earth's orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit "Earth's orbit") by definition pointing always towards the [Celestial Poles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_Poles "Celestial Poles"). Due to Earth's axial tilt, the amount of sunlight reaching any given point on the surface varies over the course of the year. This causes the seasonal change in climate, with summer in the [Northern Hemisphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere "Northern Hemisphere") occurring when the [Tropic of Cancer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer "Tropic of Cancer") is facing the Sun, and in the [Southern Hemisphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere "Southern Hemisphere") when the [Tropic of Capricorn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropic_of_Capricorn "Tropic of Capricorn") faces the Sun. In each instance, winter occurs simultaneously in the opposite hemisphere.

During the summer, the day lasts longer, and the Sun climbs higher in the sky. In winter, the climate becomes cooler and the days shorter.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-175) Above the [Arctic Circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic_Circle "Arctic Circle") and below the [Antarctic Circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctic_Circle "Antarctic Circle") there is no daylight at all for part of the year, causing a [polar night](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_night "Polar night"), and this night extends for several months at the poles themselves. These same latitudes also experience a [midnight sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Midnight_sun "Midnight sun"), where the sun remains visible all day.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-176)[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-177)

By astronomical convention, the four seasons can be determined by the solstices‚Äîthe points in the orbit of maximum axial tilt toward or away from the Sun‚Äîand the [equinoxes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equinox "Equinox"), when Earth's rotational axis is aligned with its orbital axis. In the Northern Hemisphere, [winter solstice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winter_solstice "Winter solstice") currently occurs around 21 December; [summer solstice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Summer_solstice "Summer solstice") is near 21 June, spring equinox is around 20 March and [autumnal equinox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/September_equinox "September equinox") is about 22 or 23 September. In the Southern Hemisphere, the situation is reversed, with the summer and winter solstices exchanged and the spring and autumnal equinox dates swapped.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-bromberg2008-178)

The angle of Earth's axial tilt is relatively stable over long periods of time. Its axial tilt does undergo [nutation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_nutation "Astronomical nutation"); a slight, irregular motion with a main period of 18.6 years.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-lin2006-179) The orientation (rather than the angle) of Earth's axis also changes over time, [precessing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axial_precession "Axial precession") around in a complete circle over each 25,800-year cycle; this precession is the reason for the difference between a sidereal year and a [tropical year](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropical_year "Tropical year"). Both of these motions are caused by the varying attraction of the Sun and the Moon on Earth's equatorial bulge. The poles also migrate a few meters across Earth's surface. This [polar motion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_motion "Polar motion") has multiple, cyclical components, which collectively are termed [quasiperiodic motion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quasiperiodic_motion "Quasiperiodic motion"). In addition to an annual component to this motion, there is a 14-month cycle called the [Chandler wobble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chandler_wobble "Chandler wobble"). Earth's rotational velocity also varies in a phenomenon known as length-of-day variation.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-fisher19960205-180)

Earth's annual orbit is elliptical rather than circular, and its closest approach to the Sun is called [perihelion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perihelion "Perihelion"). In modern times, Earth's perihelion occurs around 3 January, and its [aphelion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aphelion "Aphelion") around 4 July. These dates shift over time due to precession and changes to the orbit, the latter of which follows cyclical patterns known as [Milankovitch cycles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles "Milankovitch cycles"). The annual change in the Earth‚ÄìSun distance causes an increase of about 6.8% in solar energy reaching Earth at perihelion relative to aphelion.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-181)[[n 12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-solar_energy-182) Because the Southern Hemisphere is tilted toward the Sun at about the same time that Earth reaches the closest approach to the Sun, the Southern Hemisphere receives slightly more energy from the Sun than does the northern over the course of a year. This effect is much less significant than the total energy change due to the axial tilt, and most of the excess energy is absorbed by the higher proportion of water in the Southern Hemisphere.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-183)

Earth‚ÄìMoon system
-----------------

### Moon

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/MarsReconnaissanceOrbiter-Views-EarthMoon-20220422.jpg/250px-MarsReconnaissanceOrbiter-Views-EarthMoon-20220422.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:MarsReconnaissanceOrbiter-Views-EarthMoon-20220422.jpg)

Earth and the Moon as seen from [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars") by the _[Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars\_Reconnaissance\_Orbiter "Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter")_

The Moon is a relatively large, [terrestrial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrestrial_planet "Terrestrial planet"), [planet-like natural satellite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary-mass_moon "Planetary-mass moon"), with a diameter about one-quarter of Earth's. It is the largest moon in the Solar System relative to the size of its planet, although [Charon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charon_(moon) "Charon (moon)") is larger relative to the dwarf planet [Pluto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluto "Pluto").[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-184)[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-185) The natural satellites of other planets are also referred to as "moons", after Earth's.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-186) The most widely accepted theory of the Moon's origin, the [giant-impact hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giant-impact_hypothesis "Giant-impact hypothesis"), states that it formed from the collision of a Mars-size protoplanet called Theia with the early Earth. This hypothesis explains the Moon's relative lack of iron and volatile elements and the fact that its composition is nearly identical to that of Earth's crust.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-canup_asphaug2001b-44) Computer simulations suggest that two blob-like remnants of this protoplanet could be inside the Earth.[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-NYT-20231101-187)[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-NAT-20231101-188)

The gravitational attraction between Earth and the Moon causes [lunar tides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_tide "Lunar tide") on Earth.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-189) The same effect on the Moon has led to its [tidal locking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_locking "Tidal locking"): its rotation period is the same as the time it takes to orbit Earth. As a result, it always presents the same face to the planet.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-190) As the Moon orbits Earth, different parts of its face are illuminated by the Sun, leading to the [lunar phases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_phase "Lunar phase").[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-191) Due to their [tidal interaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_interactions "Tidal interactions"), the Moon recedes from Earth at the rate of approximately 38 mm/a (1.5 in/year). Over millions of years, these tiny modifications‚Äîand the lengthening of Earth's day by about 23[Œºs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsecond "Microsecond")/yr‚Äîadd up to significant changes.[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-espenak_meeus20070207-192) During the [Ediacaran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ediacaran "Ediacaran") period, for example, (approximately 620 Ma) there were 400¬±7 days in a year, with each day lasting 21.9¬±0.4 hours.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-193)

The Moon may have dramatically affected the development of life by moderating the planet's climate. [Paleontological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleontology "Paleontology") evidence and computer simulations show that Earth's axial tilt is stabilized by tidal interactions with the Moon.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-aaa428_261-194) Some theorists think that without this stabilization against the [torques](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Torque "Torque") applied by the Sun and planets to Earth's equatorial bulge, the rotational axis might be chaotically unstable, exhibiting large changes over millions of years, as is the case for Mars, though this is disputed.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-195)[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-196)

Viewed from Earth, the Moon is just far enough away to have almost the same apparent-sized disk as the Sun. The [angular size](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_size "Angular size") (or [solid angle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid_angle "Solid angle")) of these two bodies match because, although the Sun's diameter is about 400 times as large as the Moon's, it is also 400 times more distant.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-angular-170) This allows total and annular solar eclipses to occur on Earth.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-197)

### Asteroids and artificial satellites

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Debris-GEO1280.jpg/250px-Debris-GEO1280.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Debris-GEO1280.jpg)

A computer-generated image mapping the prevalence of [artificial satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_satellite "Artificial satellite") and [space debris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_debris "Space debris") around Earth in [geosynchronous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit "Geosynchronous orbit") and [low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit "Low Earth orbit")

Earth's [co-orbital asteroids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Co-orbital_configuration "Co-orbital configuration") population consists of [quasi-satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quasi-satellite "Quasi-satellite"), objects with a [horseshoe orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horseshoe_orbit "Horseshoe orbit") and [trojans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trojan_(celestial_body) "Trojan (celestial body)"). There are at least seven quasi-satellites, including [469219 Kamo ªoalewa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/469219_Kamo%CA%BBoalewa "469219 Kamo ªoalewa"), ranging in diameter from 10 m to 5000 m.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-christou_asher2011-198)[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-199) A [trojan asteroid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_trojan "Earth trojan") companion, [2010 TK 7](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2010_TK7 "2010 TK7"), is [librating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libration "Libration") around the leading [Lagrange triangular point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrangian_point "Lagrangian point"), L4, in [Earth's orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit "Earth's orbit") around the Sun.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Choi-200) The tiny [near-Earth asteroid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid "Near-Earth asteroid")[2006 RH 120](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2006_RH120 "2006 RH120") makes close approaches to the Earth‚ÄìMoon system roughly every twenty years. During these approaches, it can orbit Earth for brief periods of time.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-201)

As of September 2021, there are 4,550 operational, human-made [satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satellite "Satellite") orbiting Earth.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ucs-202) There are also inoperative satellites, including [Vanguard 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vanguard_1 "Vanguard 1"), the oldest satellite currently in orbit, and over 16,000 pieces of tracked [space debris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_debris "Space debris").[[n 13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-space_debris-203) Earth's largest artificial satellite is the [International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station") (ISS).[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-204)

Hydrosphere
-----------

[![Image 20](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Ocean_world_Earth.jpg/250px-Ocean_world_Earth.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ocean_world_Earth.jpg)

A view of Earth with its [global ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_ocean "Global ocean") and [cloud cover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cloud_cover "Cloud cover"), which dominate Earth's surface and [hydrosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrosphere "Hydrosphere"); at Earth's [polar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_regions_of_Earth "Polar regions of Earth") regions, its hydrosphere forms larger areas of ice cover.

Earth's hydrosphere is the sum of Earth's water and its distribution. Most of Earth's hydrosphere consists of Earth's global ocean. Earth's hydrosphere also consists of water in the atmosphere and on land, including clouds, inland seas, lakes, rivers, and underground waters. The mass of the oceans is approximately 1.35√ó10 18[metric tons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metric_ton "Metric ton") or about 1/4400 of Earth's total mass. The oceans cover an area of 361.8 million km 2 (139.7 million sq mi) with a mean depth of 3,682 m (12,080 ft), resulting in an estimated volume of 1.332 billion km 3 (320 million cu mi).[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ocean23_2_112-205)

If all of Earth's crustal surface were at the same elevation as a smooth sphere, the depth of the resulting world ocean would be 2.7 to 2.8 km (1.68 to 1.74 mi).[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-206) About 97.5% of the water is [saline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saline_water "Saline water"); the remaining 2.5% is [fresh water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fresh_water "Fresh water").[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-207)[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-208) Most fresh water, about 68.7%, is present as ice in [ice caps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_cap "Ice cap") and [glaciers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glacier "Glacier").[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-209) The remaining 30% is [ground water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ground_water "Ground water"), 1% [surface water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_water "Surface water") (covering only 2.8% of Earth's land)[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Lake_Scientist_2016-210) and other small forms of fresh water deposits such as [permafrost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permafrost "Permafrost"), [water vapor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vapor "Water vapor") in the atmosphere, biological binding, etc.[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-School_2019-211)[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Education_2022-212)

In Earth's coldest regions, snow survives over the summer and [changes into ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_formation "Ice formation"). This accumulated snow and ice eventually forms into [glaciers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glacier "Glacier"), bodies of ice that flow under the influence of their own gravity. [Alpine glaciers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpine_glaciers "Alpine glaciers") form in mountainous areas, whereas vast [ice sheets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_sheets "Ice sheets") form over land in polar regions. The flow of glaciers erodes the surface, changing it dramatically, with the formation of [U-shaped valleys](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U-shaped_valley "U-shaped valley") and other landforms.[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-213)[Sea ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_ice "Sea ice") in the Arctic covers an area about as big as the United States, although it is quickly retreating as a consequence of climate change.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-214)

The average [salinity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salinity "Salinity") of Earth's oceans is about 35 grams of salt per kilogram of seawater (3.5% salt).[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-kennish2001-215) Most of this salt was released from volcanic activity or extracted from cool igneous rocks.[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-mullen2002-216) The oceans are also a reservoir of dissolved atmospheric gases, which are essential for the survival of many aquatic life forms.[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-natsci_oxy4-217) Sea water has an important influence on the world's climate, with the oceans acting as a large [heat reservoir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_reservoir "Heat reservoir").[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-michon2006-218) Shifts in the oceanic temperature distribution can cause significant weather shifts, such as the [El Ni√±o‚ÄìSouthern Oscillation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o%E2%80%93Southern_Oscillation "El Ni√±o‚ÄìSouthern Oscillation").[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-sample2005-219)

The abundance of water, particularly liquid water, on Earth's surface is a unique feature that distinguishes it from other planets in the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System"). Solar System planets with considerable atmospheres do partly host atmospheric water vapor, but they lack surface conditions for stable surface water.[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Center_2021-220) Despite some [moons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_satellite "Natural satellite") showing signs of large reservoirs of [extraterrestrial liquid water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extraterrestrial_liquid_water "Extraterrestrial liquid water"), with possibly even more volume than Earth's ocean, all of them are [large bodies of water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_largest_lakes_and_seas_in_the_Solar_System "List of largest lakes and seas in the Solar System") under a kilometers thick frozen surface layer.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-221)

Atmosphere
----------

[![Image 21](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/ISS-42_Waning_sun.jpg/250px-ISS-42_Waning_sun.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS-42_Waning_sun.jpg)

A view of Earth with different layers of its atmosphere visible: the [troposphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Troposphere "Troposphere") with its clouds casting shadows, a band of [stratospheric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stratospheric "Stratospheric") blue sky at the horizon, and a line of green [airglow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airglow "Airglow") of the lower [thermosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermosphere "Thermosphere") around an [altitude of 100 km, at the edge of space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_line "K√°rm√°n line")

The [atmospheric pressure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure "Atmospheric pressure") at Earth's sea level averages 101.325 kPa (14.696 psi),[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Exline2006-222) with a [scale height](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scale_height "Scale height") of about 8.5 km (5.3 mi).[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-earth_fact_sheet-5) A dry atmosphere is composed of 78.084% [nitrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen "Nitrogen"), 20.946% oxygen, 0.934% [argon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argon "Argon"), and trace amounts of carbon dioxide and other gaseous molecules.[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Exline2006-222)[Water vapor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vapor "Water vapor") content varies between 0.01% and 4%[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Exline2006-222) but averages about 1%.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-earth_fact_sheet-5)[Clouds cover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cloud_cover "Cloud cover") around two-thirds of Earth's surface, more so over oceans than land.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-King_Platnick_Menzel_Ackerman_2013_pp._3826%E2%80%933852-223) The height of the [troposphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Troposphere "Troposphere") varies with latitude, ranging between 8 km (5 mi) at the poles to 17 km (11 mi) at the equator, with some variation resulting from weather and seasonal factors.[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-geerts_linacre97-224)

Earth's [biosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biosphere "Biosphere") has significantly altered its [atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth "Atmosphere of Earth"). [Oxygenic photosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen_evolution#Oxygen_evolution_in_nature "Oxygen evolution") evolved 2.7 Gya, [forming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen_catastrophe "Oxygen catastrophe") the primarily nitrogen‚Äìoxygen atmosphere of today.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-NYT-20131003-66) This change enabled the proliferation of [aerobic organisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aerobic_organisms "Aerobic organisms") and, indirectly, the formation of the ozone layer due to the subsequent [conversion of atmospheric O 2 into O 3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ozone%E2%80%93oxygen_cycle "Ozone‚Äìoxygen cycle"). The ozone layer blocks ultraviolet [solar radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_radiation "Solar radiation"), permitting life on land.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Harrison_2002-225) Other atmospheric functions important to life include transporting water vapor, providing useful gases, causing small meteors to burn up before they strike the surface, and moderating temperature.[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-atmosphere-226) This last phenomenon is the [greenhouse effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_effect "Greenhouse effect"): trace molecules within the atmosphere serve to capture [thermal energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_energy "Thermal energy") emitted from the surface, thereby raising the average temperature. Water vapor, carbon dioxide, [methane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methane "Methane"), [nitrous oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrous_oxide "Nitrous oxide"), and [ozone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ozone "Ozone") are the primary greenhouse gases in the atmosphere. Without this heat-retention effect, the average surface temperature would be ‚àí18¬∞C (0¬∞F), in contrast to the current +15¬∞C (59¬∞F),[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Pidwirny2006_7-227) and life on Earth probably would not exist in its current form.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Narottam2008-228)

### Weather and climate

[![Image 22](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/IntertropicalConvergenceZone-EO.jpg/330px-IntertropicalConvergenceZone-EO.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:IntertropicalConvergenceZone-EO.jpg)

The [ITCZ](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ITCZ "ITCZ")'s band of clouds over the Eastern Pacific and the Americas as seen from space

[![Image 23](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_%281980%E2%80%932016%29_no_borders.png/330px-K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_%281980%E2%80%932016%29_no_borders.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_(1980%E2%80%932016)_no_borders.png)

Earth's atmosphere has no definite boundary, gradually becoming thinner and fading into outer space.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-229) Three-quarters of the atmosphere's mass is contained within the first 11 km (6.8 mi) of the surface; this lowest layer is called the troposphere.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-230) Energy from the Sun heats this layer, and the surface below, causing expansion of the air. This lower-density air then rises and is replaced by cooler, higher-density air. The result is [atmospheric circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation "Atmospheric circulation") that drives the weather and climate through redistribution of thermal energy.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-moran2005-231)

The primary atmospheric circulation bands consist of the [trade winds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trade_winds "Trade winds") in the equatorial region below 30¬∞ latitude and the [westerlies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Westerlies "Westerlies") in the mid-latitudes between 30¬∞ and 60¬∞.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-berger2002-232)[Ocean heat content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_heat_content "Ocean heat content") and [currents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_current "Ocean current") are also important factors in determining climate, particularly the [thermohaline circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation "Thermohaline circulation") that distributes thermal energy from the equatorial oceans to the polar regions.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-rahmstorf2003-233)

Earth receives 1361 W/m 2 of[solar irradiance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_irradiance "Solar irradiance").[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-earth_fact_sheet-5)[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-234) The amount of solar energy that reaches Earth's surface decreases with increasing latitude. At higher latitudes, the sunlight reaches the surface at lower angles, and it must pass through thicker columns of the atmosphere. As a result, the mean annual air temperature at sea level decreases by about 0.4¬∞C (0.7¬∞F) per degree of latitude from the equator.[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-sadava_heller2006-235) Earth's surface can be subdivided into specific latitudinal belts of approximately homogeneous climate. Ranging from the equator to the polar regions, these are the tropical (or equatorial), [subtropical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subtropics "Subtropics"), [temperate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temperate "Temperate") and [polar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_region "Polar region") climates.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-climate_zones-236)

Further factors that affect a location's climates are its [proximity to oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continentality "Continentality"), the oceanic and atmospheric circulation, and topology.[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-237) Places close to oceans typically have colder summers and warmer winters, due to the fact that oceans can store large amounts of heat. The wind transports the cold or the heat of the ocean to the land.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-238) Atmospheric circulation also plays an important role: San Francisco and Washington DC are both coastal cities at about the same latitude. San Francisco's climate is significantly more moderate as the prevailing wind direction is from sea to land.[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-239) Finally, temperatures [decrease with height](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lapse_rate "Lapse rate") causing mountainous areas to be colder than low-lying areas.[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-240)

Water vapor generated through surface evaporation is transported by circulatory patterns in the atmosphere. When atmospheric conditions permit an uplift of warm, humid air, this water condenses and falls to the surface as [precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation").[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-moran2005-231) Most of the water is then transported to lower elevations by river systems and usually returned to the oceans or deposited into lakes. This [water cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_cycle "Water cycle") is a vital mechanism for supporting life on land and is a primary factor in the erosion of surface features over geological periods. Precipitation patterns vary widely, ranging from several meters of water per year to less than a millimeter. Atmospheric circulation, topographic features, and temperature differences determine the average precipitation that falls in each region.[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-hydrologic_cycle-241)

The commonly used [K√∂ppen climate classification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification "K√∂ppen climate classification") system has five broad groups ([humid tropics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropical_climate "Tropical climate"), [arid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arid "Arid"), [humid middle latitudes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate "Humid subtropical climate"), [continental](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continental_climate "Continental climate") and cold [polar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_climate "Polar climate")), which are further divided into more specific subtypes.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-berger2002-232) The K√∂ppen system rates regions based on observed temperature and precipitation.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-242) Surface [air temperature can rise to](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highest_temperature_recorded_on_Earth "Highest temperature recorded on Earth") around 55¬∞C (131¬∞F) in [hot deserts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hot_desert "Hot desert"), such as [Death Valley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Death_Valley_National_Park "Death Valley National Park"), and [can fall as low as](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lowest_temperature_recorded_on_Earth "Lowest temperature recorded on Earth") ‚àí89¬∞C (‚àí128¬∞F) in [Antarctica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica "Antarctica").[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-243)[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-244)

### Upper atmosphere

[![Image 24](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Antarctic_aurora_ESA313457.jpg/330px-Antarctic_aurora_ESA313457.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Antarctic_aurora_ESA313457.jpg)

Earth's night-side upper atmosphere appearing from the bottom as bands of [afterglow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afterglow "Afterglow") illuminating the [troposphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Troposphere "Troposphere") in orange with silhouettes of clouds, and the [stratosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stratosphere "Stratosphere") in white and blue. Next the [mesosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesosphere "Mesosphere") (pink area) extends to the orange and faintly green line of the lowest [airglow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airglow "Airglow"), at about one hundred kilometers at the [edge of space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_space#Boundary "Outer space") and the lower edge of the [thermosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermosphere "Thermosphere") (invisible). Continuing with green and red bands of [aurorae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurora "Aurora") stretching over several hundred kilometers.

The upper atmosphere, the atmosphere above the troposphere,[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-245) is usually divided into the [stratosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stratosphere "Stratosphere"), [mesosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesosphere "Mesosphere"), and [thermosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermosphere "Thermosphere").[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-atmosphere-226) Each layer has a different lapse rate, defining the rate of change in temperature with height. Beyond these, the [exosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exosphere "Exosphere") thins out into the magnetosphere, where the geomagnetic fields interact with the solar wind.[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-sciweek2004-246) Within the stratosphere is the ozone layer, a component that partially shields the surface from ultraviolet light and thus is important for life on Earth. The [K√°rm√°n line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_line "K√°rm√°n line"), defined as 100 km (62 mi) above Earth's surface, is a working definition for the boundary between the atmosphere and [outer space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_space "Outer space").[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-cordoba2004-247)

Thermal energy causes some of the molecules at the outer edge of the atmosphere to increase their velocity to the point where they can escape from Earth's gravity. This causes a slow but steady [loss of the atmosphere into space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_escape "Atmospheric escape"). Because unfixed [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen") has a low [molecular mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_mass "Molecular mass"), it can achieve [escape velocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Escape_velocity "Escape velocity") more readily, and it leaks into outer space at a greater rate than other gases.[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-jas31_4_1118-248) The leakage of hydrogen into space contributes to the shifting of Earth's atmosphere and surface from an initially [reducing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Redox "Redox") state to its current oxidizing one. Photosynthesis provided a source of free oxygen, but the loss of reducing agents such as hydrogen is thought to have been a necessary precondition for the widespread accumulation of oxygen in the atmosphere.[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-sci293_5531_839-249) Hence the ability of hydrogen to escape from the atmosphere may have influenced the nature of life that developed on Earth.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-abedon1997-250) In the current, oxygen-rich atmosphere most hydrogen is converted into water before it has an opportunity to escape. Instead, most of the hydrogen loss comes from the destruction of methane in the upper atmosphere.[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-arwps4_265-251)

Life on Earth
-------------

[![Image 25](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Mollweide_Cycle.gif/330px-Mollweide_Cycle.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mollweide_Cycle.gif)

An animation of the changing density of [productive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primary_production "Primary production") vegetation on land (low in brown; heavy in dark green) and phytoplankton at the [ocean surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_surface "Ocean surface") (low in purple; high in yellow)

Earth is the only known place that has ever been [habitable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability "Planetary habitability") for life. Earth's life developed in Earth's early bodies of water some hundred million years after Earth formed. Earth's life has been shaping and inhabiting many particular [ecosystems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecosystem "Ecosystem") on Earth and has eventually expanded globally forming an overarching biosphere.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-252)

Therefore, life has impacted Earth, significantly altering Earth's atmosphere and surface over long periods of time, causing changes like the [Great Oxidation Event](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Oxidation_Event "Great Oxidation Event").[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-253) Earth's life has also over time greatly diversified, allowing the biosphere to have different [biomes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biome "Biome"), which are inhabited by comparatively similar plants and animals.[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-254) The different biomes developed at distinct elevations or [water depths](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_depths "Ocean depths"), planetary temperature [latitudes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latitude "Latitude") and on land also with different [humidity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humidity "Humidity"). [Earth's species diversity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latitudinal_gradients_in_species_diversity "Latitudinal gradients in species diversity") and [biomass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biomass_(ecology) "Biomass (ecology)") reaches a peak in shallow waters and with [forests, particularly in equatorial, warm and humid conditions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropical_rainforest "Tropical rainforest"). While freezing [polar regions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_regions_of_Earth "Polar regions of Earth") and [high altitudes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpine_tundra "Alpine tundra"), or [extremely arid areas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desert "Desert") are relatively barren of plant and animal life.[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-amnat163_2_192-255)

Earth provides liquid water‚Äîan environment where complex [organic molecules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organic_compound "Organic compound") can assemble and interact, and sufficient energy to sustain a [metabolism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolism "Metabolism").[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ab2003-256) Plants and other organisms take up [nutrients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nutrient "Nutrient") from water, soils and the atmosphere. These nutrients are constantly recycled between different species.[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-257)

[![Image 26](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Desert_Electric.jpg/250px-Desert_Electric.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Desert_Electric.jpg)

A [High Desert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_Desert_(California) "High Desert (California)") storm in the [Mojave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mojave_Desert "Mojave Desert")

[Extreme weather](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extreme_weather "Extreme weather"), such as [tropical cyclones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropical_cyclone "Tropical cyclone"), occurs over most of Earth's surface and has a large impact on life in those areas. From 1980 to 2000, these events caused an average of 11,800 human deaths per year.[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-258) Many places are subject to [earthquakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earthquake "Earthquake"), [landslides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landslide "Landslide"), [tsunamis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsunami "Tsunami"), [Volcanic eruptions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanic_eruption "Volcanic eruption"), [tornadoes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tornado "Tornado"), [blizzards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blizzard "Blizzard"), [floods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flood "Flood"), [droughts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drought "Drought"), [wildfires](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wildfire "Wildfire"), and other calamities and disasters.[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-259) Human impact is felt in many areas due to pollution of the air and water, [acid rain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acid_rain "Acid rain"), loss of vegetation ([overgrazing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Overgrazing "Overgrazing"), [deforestation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deforestation "Deforestation"), [desertification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desertification "Desertification")), loss of wildlife, species [extinction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extinction "Extinction"),[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-260)[soil degradation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_degradation "Soil degradation"), [soil depletion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_depletion "Soil depletion") and [erosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erosion "Erosion").[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-261) Human activities release greenhouse gases into the atmosphere which cause [global warming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_warming "Global warming").[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ConsensusOnConsensus-262) This is driving [changes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change "Effects of climate change") such as the [melting of glaciers and ice sheets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850 "Retreat of glaciers since 1850"), a [global rise in average sea levels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_level_rise "Sea level rise"), increased risk of drought and wildfires, and migration of species to colder areas.[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Global_Warming_Effects-263)

Human geography
---------------

[![Image 27](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/BlackMarble20161km.jpg/330px-BlackMarble20161km.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:BlackMarble20161km.jpg)

A composite image of [artificial light emissions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light_pollution "Light pollution") at night on a map of Earth

Humans, who originated from earlier [primates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primate "Primate") in Eastern Africa 300,000 years ago, [have since been migrating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_human_migration "History of human migration") around Earth, and with the advent of agriculture in the 10th millennium BC, have been increasingly [settling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sedentism "Sedentism") Earth's land.[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-264) In the 20th century, [Antarctica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica "Antarctica") became the last continent to be explored and settled by humans, although human presence there remains limited.

Since the 19th century, [human population](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_population "World population") has grown exponentially to eight billion in the 2020s,[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-265) and is projected to peak at around ten billion in the second half of the 21st century.[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Harvey-2020-266) Most of the growth is expected to take place in [sub-Saharan Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa").[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Harvey-2020-266)

Distribution and [density of human population](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_density#Human_population_density "Population density") varies greatly around the world with the majority living in south to eastern Asia and 90% inhabiting the [Northern Hemisphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere "Northern Hemisphere") of Earth,[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-267) partly due to the [hemispherical predominance of the world's land mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_hemisphere "Land hemisphere"), with 68% of the world's land mass being in the Northern Hemisphere.[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-268) Furthermore, since the 19th century humans have increasingly converged into urban areas, with the majority living in urban areas by the 21st century.[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-OWID_urbanization_2019-269)

Beyond Earth's surface, humans have lived only in a few special-purpose deep [underground](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Underground_living "Underground living") and [underwater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Underwater_living "Underwater living") presences and a few [space stations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_station "Space station"). The human population virtually completely remains on Earth's surface, fully depending on Earth and the environment it sustains. Since the second half of the 20th century, some hundreds of humans have temporarily [stayed beyond Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_space_travellers_by_first_flight "List of space travellers by first flight"), a tiny fraction of whom have reached another celestial body, the Moon.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-shayler_vis2005-270)[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-271)

Earth has been subject to extensive human settlement, and humans have developed diverse societies and cultures. Most of Earth's land has been territorially claimed since the 19th century by [sovereign states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sovereign_state "Sovereign state") (countries) separated by [political borders](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Border "Border"), and [205 such states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states "List of sovereign states") exist today,[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-272) with only parts of Antarctica and a few small regions [remaining unclaimed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terra_nullius "Terra nullius").[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-273) Together, most of these states form the United Nations, the leading worldwide [intergovernmental organization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intergovernmental_organization "Intergovernmental organization"),[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-274) which extends human governance [over the ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_the_Sea "Law of the Sea") and [Antarctica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_System "Antarctic Treaty System"), and therefore all of Earth.

### Natural resources and land use

[![Image 28](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Global_land_use_for_food_production%2C_OWID_statistic.png/330px-Global_land_use_for_food_production%2C_OWID_statistic.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Global_land_use_for_food_production,_OWID_statistic.png)

Earth's land use for human agriculture in 2019

Earth has resources that have been exploited by humans.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-275) Those termed [non-renewable resources](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-renewable_resource "Non-renewable resource"), such as [fossil fuels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil_fuel "Fossil fuel"), are only replenished over geological timescales.[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-276) Large deposits of fossil fuels are obtained from Earth's crust, consisting of coal, petroleum, and natural gas.[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-277) These deposits are used by humans both for energy production and as feedstock for chemical production.[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-278) Mineral [ore](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ore "Ore") bodies have also been formed within the crust through a process of [ore genesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ore_genesis "Ore genesis"), resulting from actions of [magmatism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magmatism "Magmatism"), erosion, and plate tectonics.[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-279) These metals and other elements are extracted by mining, a process which often brings environmental and health damage.[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-280)

Earth's biosphere produces many useful biological products for humans, including food, wood, [pharmaceuticals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pharmaceutical "Pharmaceutical"), oxygen, and the recycling of organic waste. The land-based ecosystem depends upon [topsoil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topsoil "Topsoil") and fresh water, and the oceanic ecosystem depends on dissolved nutrients washed down from the land.[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-science299_5607_673-281) In 2019, 39 million km 2 (15 million sq mi) of Earth's land surface consisted of forest and woodlands, 12 million km 2 (4.6 million sq mi) was shrub and grassland, 40 million km 2 (15 million sq mi) were used for animal feed production and grazing, and 11 million km 2 (4.2 million sq mi) were cultivated as croplands.[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-OWID_2019-282) Of the 12‚Äì14% of ice-free land that is used for croplands, 2 [percentage points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Percentage_point "Percentage point") were irrigated in 2015.[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-283) Humans use [building materials](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Building_material "Building material") to construct shelters.[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-284)

### Humans and the environment

[![Image 29: The graph from 1880 to 2020 shows natural drivers exhibiting fluctuations of about 0.3 degrees Celsius. Human drivers steadily increase by 0.3 degrees over 100 years to 1980, then steeply by 0.8 degrees more over the past 40 years.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg/330px-Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg)

Change in average surface air temperature and drivers for that change. Human activity has caused increased temperatures, with natural forces adding some variability.[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-285)

Human activities have impacted Earth's environments. Through activities such as the burning of fossil fuels, humans have been increasing the amount of [greenhouse gases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas "Greenhouse gas") in the atmosphere, altering [Earth's energy budget](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_budget "Earth's energy budget") and climate.[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-ConsensusOnConsensus-262)[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-286) It is estimated that global temperatures in the year 2020 were 1.2¬∞C (2.2¬∞F) warmer than the preindustrial baseline.[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-287) This increase in temperature, known as [global warming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_warming "Global warming"), has contributed to the [melting of glaciers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850 "Retreat of glaciers since 1850"), [rising sea levels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_level_rise "Sea level rise"), increased risk of drought and wildfires, and migration of species to colder areas.[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Global_Warming_Effects-263)

The concept of [planetary boundaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_boundaries "Planetary boundaries") was introduced to quantify humanity's impact on Earth. Of the nine identified boundaries, five have been crossed: [Biosphere integrity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biodiversity_loss "Biodiversity loss"), climate change, chemical pollution, destruction of wild habitats and the [nitrogen cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle "Nitrogen cycle") are thought to have passed the safe threshold.[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Boundaries-288)[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-289) As of 2018, no country meets the basic needs of its population without transgressing planetary boundaries. It is thought possible to provide all basic physical needs globally within sustainable levels of resource use.[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-290)

Cultural and historical viewpoint
---------------------------------

[![Image 30: Woman seeing the Earth from space through a window](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg/250px-Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg)

[Tracy Caldwell Dyson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson "Tracy Caldwell Dyson"), a [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") astronaut, observing Earth from the [_Cupola_ module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cupola_(ISS_module) "Cupola (ISS module)") at the [International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station") on 11 September 2010

[Human cultures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Culture "Culture") have developed many views of the planet.[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-NYT-20181224b-291) The standard [astronomical symbols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_symbols "Astronomical symbols") of Earth are a quartered circle, [![Image 31: üú®](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Earth_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/16px-Earth_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Earth_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "üú®"),[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-liungman2004-292) representing the [four corners of the world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Four_corners_of_the_world "Four corners of the world"), and a [globus cruciger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Globus_cruciger "Globus cruciger"), [![Image 32: ‚ôÅ](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Globus_cruciger_%28fixed_width%29.svg/16px-Globus_cruciger_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Globus_cruciger_(fixed_width).svg "‚ôÅ"). Earth is sometimes [personified](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Personification "Personification") as a [deity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deity "Deity"). In many cultures it is a [mother goddess](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mother_goddess "Mother goddess") that is also the primary [fertility deity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fertility_deity "Fertility deity").[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Stookey-2004-293)[Creation myths](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Creation_myth "Creation myth") in many religions involve the creation of Earth by a supernatural deity or deities.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-Stookey-2004-293) The [Gaia hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis "Gaia hypothesis"), developed in the mid-20th century, compared Earth's environments and life as a single self-regulating organism leading to broad stabilization of the conditions of habitability.[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-294)[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-295)[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-296)

[Images of Earth taken from space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_first_images_of_Earth_from_space "Timeline of first images of Earth from space"), particularly during the Apollo program, have been credited with altering the way that people viewed the planet that they lived on, called the [overview effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Overview_effect "Overview effect"), emphasizing its beauty, uniqueness and apparent fragility.[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-297)[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-298) In particular, this caused a realization of the scope of effects from human activity on Earth's environment. Enabled by science, particularly [Earth observation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_observation "Earth observation"),[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-299) humans have started to take [action on environmental issues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmentalism "Environmentalism") globally,[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-300) acknowledging the impact of humans and the [interconnectedness of Earth's environments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecological_network "Ecological network").[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-301)

Scientific investigation has resulted in several culturally transformative shifts in people's view of the planet. Initial belief in a [flat Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flat_Earth "Flat Earth") was gradually displaced in [Ancient Greece](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greece "Ancient Greece") by the idea of a [spherical Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_Earth "Spherical Earth"), which was attributed to both the philosophers [Pythagoras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pythagoras "Pythagoras") and [Parmenides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parmenides "Parmenides").[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-302)[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-303) Earth was generally believed to be [the center of the universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geocentrism "Geocentrism") until the 16th century, when scientists first concluded that it was [a moving object](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliocentrism "Heliocentrism"), one of the planets of the Solar System.[[291]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-arnett20060716-304)

It was only during the 19th century that geologists realized [Earth's age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_age "Earth's age") was at least many millions of years.[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-305)[Lord Kelvin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lord_Kelvin "Lord Kelvin") used [thermodynamics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermodynamics "Thermodynamics") to estimate the age of Earth to be between 20 million and 400 million years in 1864, sparking a vigorous debate on the subject; it was only when radioactivity and [radioactive dating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiometric_dating "Radiometric dating") were discovered in the late 19th and early 20th centuries that a reliable mechanism for determining Earth's age was established, proving the planet to be billions of years old.[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-306)[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth#cite_note-307)
